<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lord_Buddha_-_Hair_Relics.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hair Relics of Buddha on display at <a href="/wiki/Gangaramaya_Temple" title="Gangaramaya Temple">Gangaramaya Temple</a> (<a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While in the Buddha's days he was addressed by the very respected titles Buddha, Shākyamuni, Shākyasimha, Bhante and Bho, he was known after his parinirvana as Arihant, Bhagavā/Bhagavat/Bhagwān, Mahāvira,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKatz198222_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKatz198222-130">[99]</a></sup> Jina/Jinendra, Sāstr, Sugata, and most popularly in scriptures as Tathāgata.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relics">Relics</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aar%C4%ABra" title="Śarīra">Śarīra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Relics_associated_with_Buddha" title="Relics associated with Buddha">Relics associated with Buddha</a></div>
<p>After his death, Buddha's cremation relics were divided amongst 8 royal families and his disciples; centuries later they would be enshrined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Ashoka" title="King Ashoka">King Ashoka</a> into 84,000 stupas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[web 20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrong2007136.E2.80.9337_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrong2007136.E2.80.9337-132">[100]</a></sup> Many supernatural legends surround the history of alleged relics as they accompanied the spread of Buddhism and gave legitimacy to rulers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Physical_characteristics_of_the_Buddha" title="Physical characteristics of the Buddha">Physical characteristics of the Buddha</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="2592" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg/170px-Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg/255px-Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg/340px-Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_Victoria_%26_Albert.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhāran</a> depiction of the Buddha from <a href="/wiki/Hadda,_Afghanistan" title="Hadda, Afghanistan">Hadda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>; Victoria and Albert Museum, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An extensive and colorful physical description of the Buddha has been laid down in scriptures. A <a href="/wiki/Kshatriya" title="Kshatriya">kshatriya</a> by birth, he had military training in his upbringing, and by Shakyan tradition was required to pass tests to demonstrate his worthiness as a warrior in order to marry.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He had a strong enough body to be noticed by one of the kings and was asked to join his army as a general.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> He is also believed by Buddhists to have "the 32 Signs of the Great Man".</p>
<p>The Brahmin Sonadanda described him as "handsome, good-looking, and pleasing to the eye, with a most beautiful complexion. He has a godlike form and countenance, he is by no means unattractive." (D, I:115)</p>
<p>"It is wonderful, truly marvellous, how serene is the good Gotama's appearance, how clear and radiant his complexion, just as the golden jujube in autumn is clear and radiant, just as a palm-tree fruit just loosened from the stalk is clear and radiant, just as an adornment of red gold wrought in a crucible by a skilled goldsmith, deftly beaten and laid on a yellow-cloth shines, blazes and glitters, even so, the good Gotama's senses are calmed, his complexion is clear and radiant." (A, I:181)</p>
<p>A disciple named Vakkali, who later became an arahant, was so obsessed by the Buddha's physical presence that the Buddha is said to have felt impelled to tell him to desist, and to have reminded him that he should know the Buddha through the Dhamma and not through physical appearances.</p>
<p>Although there are no extant representations of the Buddha in human form until around the 1st century CE (see <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_art" title="Buddhist art">Buddhist art</a>), descriptions of the physical characteristics of fully enlightened buddhas are attributed to the Buddha in the <a href="/wiki/Digha_Nikaya" title="Digha Nikaya">Digha Nikaya</a>'s <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">Lakkhaṇa Sutta</span></i> (D, I:142).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995441.E2.80.9360_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalshe1995441.E2.80.9360-133">[101]</a></sup> In addition, the Buddha's physical appearance is described by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yasodhara" title="Yasodhara">Yasodhara</a> to their son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rahula" title="Rahula">Rahula</a> upon the Buddha's first post-Enlightenment return to his former princely palace in the non-canonical Pali devotional hymn, <i>Narasīha Gāthā</i> ("The Lion of Men").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[web 21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the 32 main characteristics it is mentioned that Buddha has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein2003200_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein2003200-135">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nine_virtues">Nine virtues</span></h2>
<p>Recollection of nine virtues attributed to the Buddha is a common Buddhist meditation and devotional practice called <a href="/wiki/Buddh%C4%81nusm%E1%B9%9Bti" title="Buddhānusmṛti">Buddhānusmṛti</a>. The nine virtues are also among the <a href="/wiki/Kamma%E1%B9%AD%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na" title="Kammaṭṭhāna">40 Buddhist meditation subjects</a>. The nine virtues of the Buddha appear throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tripitaka" title="Tripitaka">Tipitaka</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[web 22]</a></sup> and include:</p>
<dl>
<dd><i>Buddho</i> – <a href="/wiki/Bodhi" title="Bodhi">Awakened</a></dd>
<dd><i>Sammasambuddho</i> – <a href="/wiki/Buddhahood#Samyaksambuddha" title="Buddhahood">Perfectly self-awakened</a></dd>
<dd><i>Vijja-carana-sampano</i> – Endowed with higher knowledge and ideal conduct.</dd>
<dd><i>Sugato</i> – Well-gone or Well-spoken.</dd>
<dd><i>Lokavidu</i> – Wise in the knowledge of the <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology" title="Buddhist cosmology">many worlds</a>.</dd>
<dd><i>Anuttaro Purisa-damma-sarathi</i> – Unexcelled trainer of untrained people.</dd>
<dd><i>Satthadeva-Manussanam</i> – Teacher of <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Buddhism)" title="Deva (Buddhism)">gods</a> and humans.</dd>
<dd><i>Bhagavathi</i> – <a href="/wiki/Bhagavan" title="Bhagavan">The Blessed one</a></dd>
<dd><i>Araham</i> – Worthy of homage. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arahant" title="Arahant">Arahant</a> is "one with taints destroyed, who has lived the holy life, done what had to be done, laid down the burden, reached the true goal, destroyed the fetters of being, and is completely liberated through final knowledge."</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Teachings">Teachings</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="922" data-file-width="640" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg/150px-Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg/225px-Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg/300px-Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_at_pandav_cave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lord Buddha at Pandavleni Caves, <a href="/wiki/Nashik" title="Nashik">Nashik</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_philosophy" title="Buddhist philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Brahmanical_motifs">Use of Brahmanical motifs</span></h3>
<p>In the Pali Canon, the Buddha uses many <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Brahmanical</a> devices. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Samyutta_Nikaya" title="Samyutta Nikaya">Samyutta Nikaya</a> 111, <a href="/wiki/Majjhima_Nikaya" title="Majjhima Nikaya">Majjhima Nikaya</a> 92 and Vinaya i 246 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a>, the Buddha praises the <a href="/wiki/Agnihotra" title="Agnihotra">Agnihotra</a> as the foremost sacrifice and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gayatri_mantra" title="Gayatri mantra">Gayatri mantra</a> as the foremost meter:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>aggihuttamukhā yaññā sāvittī chandaso mukham.</i></p>
<p>Sacrifices have the agnihotra as foremost; of meter the foremost is the Sāvitrī.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShults2014119_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShults2014119-137">[103]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tracing_the_oldest_teachings">Tracing the oldest teachings</span></h3>
<p>Information of the oldest teachings may be obtained by analysis of the oldest texts. One method to obtain information on the oldest core of Buddhism is to compare the oldest extant versions of the Theravadin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pali_Canon" title="Pali Canon">Pali Canon</a> and other texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[note 12]</a></sup> The reliability of these sources, and the possibility to draw out a core of oldest teachings, is a matter of dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-142">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1990_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1990-143">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-144">[109]</a></sup> According to Vetter, inconsistencies remain, and other methods must be applied to resolve those inconsistencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix-138">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[note 13]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Schmithausen, three positions held by scholars of Buddhism can be distinguished:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii-148">[112]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>"Stress on the fundamental homogeneity and substantial authenticity of at least a considerable part of the Nikayic materials;"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[note 14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[note 15]</a></sup>, from the oldest extant texts a common kernel can be drawn out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder1999inside_flap_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder1999inside_flap-151">[113]</a></sup> According to Warder, c.q. his publisher: "This kernel of doctrine is presumably common Buddhism of the period before the great schisms of the fourth and third centuries BC. It may be substantially the Buddhism of the Buddha himself, although this cannot be proved: at any rate it is a Buddhism presupposed by the schools as existing about a hundred years after the parinirvana of the Buddha, and there is no evidence to suggest that it was formulated by anyone else than the Buddha and his immediate followers"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder1999inside_flap_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder1999inside_flap-151">[113]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Gombrich" title="Richard Gombrich">Richard Gombrich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993viii_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993viii-152">[114]</a></sup> Richard Gombrich: "I have the greatest difficulty in accepting that the main edifice is not the work of a single genius. By "the main edifice" I mean the collections of the main body of sermons, the four Nikāyas, and of the main body of monastic rules."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_144-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-144">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Scepticism with regard to the possibility of retrieving the doctrine of earliest Buddhism;"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[note 16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[note 17]</a></sup></li>
<li>"Cautious optimism in this respect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[note 18]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dhyana_and_insight"><i>Dhyana</i> and insight</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="430" height="358" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg/220px-Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg/330px-Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kanishka_Buddha_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Buddha on a coin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanishka_I" title="Kanishka I">Kanishka I</a>, circa 130 CE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A core problem in the study of early Buddhism is the relation between <i>dhyana</i> and insight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-142">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_144-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-144">[109]</a></sup> Schmithausen notes that the mention of the four noble truths as constituting "liberating insight", which is attained after mastering the Rupa Jhanas, is a later addition to texts such as Majjhima Nikaya 36.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981-145">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-142">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Earliest_Buddhism">Earliest Buddhism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presectarian_Buddhism" title="Presectarian Buddhism">Presectarian Buddhism</a></div>
<p>According to Tilmann Vetter, the core of earliest Buddhism is the practice of <i><a href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">dhyāna</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXX.3B_XXXV-XXXVI.3B_4-5_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXX.3B_XXXV-XXXVI.3B_4-5-159">[118]</a></sup> as a workable alternative to painful ascetic practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1884_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1884-160">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[note 19]</a></sup> Bronkhorst agrees that <i>Dhyāna</i> was a Buddhist invention,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> whereas Norman notes that "the Buddha's way to release [...] was by means of meditative practices."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199729_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199729-163">[121]</a></sup> Discriminating insight into transiency as a separate path to liberation was a later development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxxiv-xxxvii_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xxxiv-xxxvii-164">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997131_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997131-165">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Mahāsaccakasutta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[note 20]</a></sup> from the fourth <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhyana_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyana in Buddhism">jhana</a></i> the Buddha gained <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhi" title="Bodhi">bodhi</a></i>. Yet, it is not clear what he was awakened to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199729_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199729-163">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> According to Schmithausen and Bronkhorst, "liberating insight" is a later addition to this text, and reflects a later development and understanding in early Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981-145">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The mentioning of the four truths as constituting "liberating insight" introduces a logical problem, since the four truths depict a linear path of practice, the knowledge of which is in itself not depicted as being liberating:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter19885_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter19885-167">[124]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[T]hey do not teach that one is released by knowing the four noble truths, but by practicing the fourth noble truth, the eightfold path, which culminates in right samadhi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter19885_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter19885-167">[124]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nibbana" title="Nibbana">"Nibbāna"</a></i> (Sanskrit: <i>Nirvāna</i>) is the common term for the desired goal of this practice, many other terms can be found throughout the Nikayas, which are not specified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xv_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xv-168">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[note 21]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Vetter, the description of the Buddhist path may initially have been as simple as the term "the middle way".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXVIII_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXVIII-170">[126]</a></sup> In time, this short description was elaborated, resulting in the description of the eightfold path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXVIII_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXVIII-170">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to both Bronkhorst and Anderson, the <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">four truths</a> became a substitution for prajna, or "liberating insight", in the suttas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst199399-100.2C_102-111_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst199399-100.2C_102-111-117">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAnderson1999_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAnderson1999-113">[87]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2015)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> in those texts where "liberating insight" was preceded by the four jhanas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993108_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993108-171">[127]</a></sup> According to Bronkhorst, the four truths may not have been formulated in earliest Buddhism, and did not serve in earliest Buddhism as a description of "liberating insight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993107_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993107-172">[128]</a></sup> Gotama's teachings may have been personal, "adjusted to the need of each person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993108_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993108-171">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Three_marks_of_existence" title="Three marks of existence">three marks of existence</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[note 22]</a></sup> may reflect Upanishadic or other influences. K.R. Norman supposes that these terms were already in use at the Buddha's time, and were familiar to his listeners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199726_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199726-174">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Brahmavihara" title="Brahmavihara">Brahma-vihara</a> was in origin probably a brahmanic term;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199728_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199728-175">[130]</a></sup> but its usage may have been common to the Sramana traditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_developments">Later developments</span></h3>
<p>In time, "liberating insight" became an essential feature of the Buddhist tradition. The following teachings, which are commonly seen as essential to Buddhism, are later formulations which form part of the explanatory framework of this "liberating insight":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_142-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-142">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths" title="Four Noble Truths">Four Noble Truths</a>: that suffering is an ingrained part of existence; that the origin of suffering is craving for sensuality, acquisition of identity, and fear of annihilation; that suffering can be ended; and that following the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a> is the means to accomplish this;</li>
<li>The Noble Eightfold Path: right view, right intention, right speech, right action, right livelihood, right effort, right mindfulness, and right concentration;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratitya-samutpada" title="Pratitya-samutpada">Dependent origination</a>: the mind creates suffering as a natural product of a complex process.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_religions">Other religions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avatarbuddha.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="273" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Avatarbuddha.jpg/220px-Avatarbuddha.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Avatarbuddha.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avatarbuddha.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Buddha depicted as the 9th <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> of god <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> in a traditional Hindu representation</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha_in_world_religions" title="Gautama Buddha in world religions">Gautama Buddha in world religions</a></div>
<p>Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a> regard Gautama as the 9th <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google260_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-google260-85">[note 8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGopal199073_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGopal199073-176">[131]</a></sup> However, Buddha's teachings deny the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">Brahman</a>-<a href="/wiki/%C4%80tman_(Hinduism)" title="Ātman (Hinduism)">Atman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-sep-177">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[134]</a></sup> Consequently Buddhism is generally classified as a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nastika" title="Nastika">nāstika</a></i> school (heterodox, literally "It is not so"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[note 23]</a></sup>) in contrast to the six orthodox schools of Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231.E2.80.93232_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlood1996231.E2.80.93232-185">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Buddha is regarded as a <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> by the minority <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Islam_and_the_Ahmadiyya_jama.CA.BBat_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Islam_and_the_Ahmadiyya_jama.CA.BBat-186">[web 23]</a></sup> sect of Muslims – a sect considered a deviant and rejected as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostate" title="Apostate">apostate</a> by mainstream Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[141]</a></sup> Some early Chinese Taoist-Buddhists thought the Buddha to be a reincarnation of <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETwitchett1986_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETwitchett1986-189">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Disciples of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cao_%C4%90%C3%A0i" title="Cao Đài">Cao Đài</a> religion worship the Buddha as a major religious teacher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJanet20123_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJanet20123-190">[143]</a></sup> His image can be found in both their Holy See and on the home altar. He is revealed during communication with Divine Beings as son of their Supreme Being (God the Father) together with other major religious teachers and founders like <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJanet20129_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJanet20129-191">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Christian <a href="/wiki/Barlaam_and_Josaphat" title="Barlaam and Josaphat">Saint Josaphat</a> is based on the Buddha. The name comes from the Sanskrit <i><a href="/wiki/Bodhisattva" title="Bodhisattva">Bodhisattva</a></i> via Arabic <i>Būdhasaf</i> and Georgian <i>Iodasaph</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacdonnel1900_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacdonnel1900-192">[145]</a></sup> The only story in which St. Josaphat appears, <i>Barlaam and Josaphat</i>, is based on the life of the Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMershman1913_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMershman1913-193">[146]</a></sup> Josaphat was included in earlier editions of the Roman Martyrology (feast day 27 November) — though not in the Roman Missal — and in the Eastern Orthodox Church liturgical calendar (26 August).</p>
<p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnostic</a> sect of <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, the Buddha is listed among the prophets who preached the word of God before <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[147]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="-moz-column-count: 3; -webkit-column-count: 3; column-count: 3; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-birthplace-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-birthplace_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birthplace_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birthplace_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birthplace_3-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-birthplace_3-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Buddhist tradition, following the <i>Nidanakatha</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[web 7]</a></sup> the introductory to the <a href="/wiki/Jataka_tales" title="Jataka tales">Jataka tales</a>, the stories of the former lives of the Buddha, Gautama was born in <a href="/wiki/Lumbini" title="Lumbini">Lumbini</a>, present-day Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[web 8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuddhistPilgrimageSites_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuddhistPilgrimageSites-49">[web 9]</a></sup> In the mid-3rd century BCE the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a> determined that Lumbini was Gautama's birthplace and thus installed a pillar there with the inscription: "...this is where the Buddha, sage of the Śākyas (<i>Śākyamuni</i>), was born."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199819_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199819-50">[35]</a></sup><br/>
<br/>
Based on stone inscriptions, there is also speculation that Lumbei, Kapileswar village, <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, at the east coast of India, was the site of ancient Lumbini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMah.C4.81p.C4.81tra1977_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMah.C4.81p.C4.81tra1977-51">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoh.C4.81p.C4.81tra2000114_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoh.C4.81p.C4.81tra2000114-52">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathy2014_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathy2014-53">[38]</a></sup> Hartmann discusses the hypothesis and states, "The inscription has generally been considered spurious (...)"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartmann199138.E2.80.9339_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartmann199138.E2.80.9339-54">[39]</a></sup> He quotes Sircar: "There can hardly be any doubt that the people responsible for the Kapilesvara inscription copied it from the said facsimile not much earlier than 1928."<br/>
<br/>
Kapilavastu was the place where he grew up:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-55">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[a]</a></sup></span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">Warder: "The Buddha [...] was born in the Sakya Republic, which was the city state of Kapilavastu, a very small state just inside the modern state boundary of Nepal against the Northern Indian frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder200045_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder200045-9">[4]</a></sup></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Walsh: "He belonged to the Sakya clan dwelling on the edge of the Himalayas, his actual birthplace being a few miles north of the present-day Northern Indian border, in Nepal. His father was in fact an elected chief of the clan rather than the king he was later made out to be, though his title was <i>raja</i> – a term which only partly corresponds to our word 'king'. Some of the states of North India at that time were kingdoms and others republics, and the Sakyan republic was subject to the powerful king of neighbouring Kosala, which lay to the south".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWalsh199520_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalsh199520-58">[42]</a></sup></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">The exact location of ancient Kapilavastu is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-55">[40]</a></sup> It may have been either <a href="/wiki/Piprahwa" title="Piprahwa">Piprahwa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, northern India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENakamura198018_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENakamura198018-59">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESrivastava197961.E2.80.9374_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava197961.E2.80.9374-60">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESrivastava1980108_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava1980108-61">[45]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Tilaurakot" title="Tilaurakot">Tilaurakot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETuladhar20021.E2.80.937_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETuladhar20021.E2.80.937-62">[46]</a></sup> present-day <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-63">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeownPrebish2013436-55">[40]</a></sup> The two cities are located only fifteen miles from each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuntington1986-63">[47]</a></sup></span></li>
</ul>
<span class="reference-text">See also <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha#Conception_and_birth" title="Gautama Buddha">Conception and birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha#Birthplace_Sources" title="Gautama Buddha">Birthplace Sources</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-deathplace-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-deathplace_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/Mahaparinibbana_Sutta" title="Mahaparinibbana Sutta">Mahaparinibbana Sutta</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[web 1]</a></sup> Gautama died in Kushinagar, which is located in present day <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Baroni: "The sage of the Shakya people"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaroni2002230_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaroni2002230-6">[3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-dating-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-dating_10-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-dating_10-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">411–400: <a href="#CITEREFDundas2002">Dundas 2002</a>, p. 24: "...as is now almost universally accepted by informed Indological scholarship, a re-examination of early Buddhist historical material, [...], necessitates a redating of the Buddha's death to between 411 and 400 BCE..."</span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">405: Richard Gombrich<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1992_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1992-35">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENarain1993187.E2.80.93201_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENarain1993187.E2.80.93201-36">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHartmann1991_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartmann1991-37">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich2000_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich2000-38">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text">Around 400: See the consensus in the essays by leading scholars in <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFNarain2003"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awadh_Kishore_Narain" title="Awadh Kishore Narain">Narain, Awadh Kishore</a>, ed. (2003), <i>The Date of the Historical Śākyamuni Buddha</i>, New Delhi: BR Publishing, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/81-7646-353-1" title="Special:BookSources/81-7646-353-1">81-7646-353-1</a></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGautama+Buddha&amp;rft.btitle=The+Date+of+the+Historical+%C5%9A%C4%81kyamuni+Buddha&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=81-7646-353-1&amp;rft.place=New+Delhi&amp;rft.pub=BR+Publishing&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>.</span></li>
<li>According to Pali scholar <a href="/wiki/K._R._Norman" title="K. R. Norman">K. R. Norman</a>, a life span for the Buddha of c. 480 to 400 BCE (and his teaching period roughly from c. 445 to 400 BCE) "fits the archaeological evidence better".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman199733_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman199733-2">[2]</a></sup> See also <a class="external text" href="http://isites.harvard.edu/fs/docs/icb.topic138396.files/Buddha-Dates.pdf" rel="nofollow">Notes on the Dates of the Buddha Íåkyamuni</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Alexander Berzin, "Buddhism developed as a shramana school that accepted rebirth under the force of karma, while rejecting the existence of the type of soul that other schools asserted. In addition, the Buddha accepted as parts of the path to liberation the use of logic and reasoning, as well as ethical behavior, but not to the degree of Jain asceticism. In this way, Buddhism avoided the extremes of the previous four shramana schools."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[web 3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 2013, archaeologist Robert Coningham found the remains of a <i>Bodhigara</i>, a tree shrine, dated to 550 BCE at the <a href="/wiki/Maya_Devi_Temple,_Lumbini" title="Maya Devi Temple, Lumbini">Maya Devi Temple, Lumbini</a>, speculating that it may possible be a Buddhist shrine. If so, this may push back the Buddha's birth date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-41">[web 4]</a></sup> Archaeologists caution that the shrine may represent pre-Buddhist tree worship, and that further research is needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-41">[web 4]</a></sup><br/>
Richard Gombrich has dismissed Coningham's speculations as "a fantasy", noting that Coningham lacks the necessary expertise on the history of early Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[web 5]</a></sup><br/>
Geoffrey Samuels notes that several locations of both early Buddhism and Jainism are closely related to <a href="/wiki/Yaksha" title="Yaksha">Yaksha</a>-worship, that several Yakshas were "converted" to Buddhism, a well-known example being <a href="/wiki/Vajrapani" title="Vajrapani">Vajrapani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[web 6]</a></sup> and that several Yaksha-shrines, where trees were worshipped, were converted into Buddhist holy places.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuels2010140.E2.80.9352_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuels2010140.E2.80.9352-44">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Minor Rock Edict Nb3: "These Dhamma texts – Extracts from the Discipline, the Noble Way of Life, the Fears to Come, the Poem on the Silent Sage, the Discourse on the Pure Life, Upatisa's Questions, and the Advice to Rahula which was spoken by the Buddha concerning false speech – these Dhamma texts, reverend sirs, I desire that all the monks and nuns may constantly listen to and remember. Likewise the laymen and laywomen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993-64">[48]</a></sup><br/>
<br/>
Dhammika:"There is disagreement amongst scholars concerning which Pali suttas correspond to some of the text. Vinaya samukose: probably the Atthavasa Vagga, Anguttara Nikaya, 1:98-100. Aliya vasani: either the Ariyavasa Sutta, Anguttara Nikaya, V:29, or the Ariyavamsa Sutta, Anguttara Nikaya, II: 27-28. Anagata bhayani: probably the Anagata Sutta, Anguttara Nikaya, III:100. Muni gatha: Muni Sutta, Sutta Nipata 207-221. Upatisa pasine: Sariputta Sutta, Sutta Nipata 955-975. Laghulavade: Rahulavada Sutta, Majjhima Nikaya, I:421."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDhammika1993-64">[48]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-google260-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-google260_85-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-google260_85-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Kumar Singh, Nagendra (1997). "Buddha as depicted in the Purāṇas". <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=UG9-HZ5icQ4C&amp;pg=PA260" rel="nofollow"><i>Encyclopaedia of Hinduism</i></a>. <b>7</b>. Anmol Publications. pp. 260–75. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-7488-168-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-81-7488-168-7">978-81-7488-168-7</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">16 April</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGautama+Buddha&amp;rft.atitle=Buddha+as+depicted+in+the+Pur%C4%81%E1%B9%87as&amp;rft.aufirst=Nagendra&amp;rft.aulast=Kumar+Singh&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopaedia+of+Hinduism&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DUG9-HZ5icQ4C%26pg%3DPA260&amp;rft.isbn=978-81-7488-168-7&amp;rft.pages=260-75&amp;rft.pub=Anmol+Publications&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to Geoffrey Samuel, the Buddha was born as a Kshatriya,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel2010_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel2010-89">[67]</a></sup> in a moderate Vedic culture at the central Ganges Plain area, where the shramana-traditions developed. This area had a moderate Vedic culture, where the kshatriyas were the highest <a href="/wiki/Varna" title="Varna">varna</a>, in contrast to the Brahmanic ideology of <a href="/wiki/Kuru_Kingdom" title="Kuru Kingdom">Kuru</a>-<a href="/wiki/Panchala" title="Panchala">Panchala</a>, were the Brahmins had become the highest varna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel2010_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel2010-89">[67]</a></sup> Both the Vedic culture and the shramana tradition contributed to the emergence of the so-called <a href="/wiki/Hinduism#Roots_of_Hinduism" title="Hinduism">"Hindu-synthesis"</a> around the start of the Common Era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel2002_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHiltebeitel2002-90">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESamuel2010_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESamuel2010-89">[67]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Scholars have noted inconsistencies in the presentations of the Buddha's enlightenment, and the Buddhist path to liberation, in the oldest sutras. These inconsistencies show that the Buddhist teachings evolved, either during the lifetime of the Buddha, or there-after.See:<br/>
* Andre Bareau (1963), <i>Recherches sur la biographiedu Buddha dans les Sutrapitaka et les Vinayapitaka anciens</i>, Ecole Francaise d'Extreme-Orient<br/>
* Schmithausen, <i>On some Aspects of Descriptions or Theories of 'Liberating Insight' and 'Enlightenment' in Early Buddhism</i><br/>
* K.R. Norman, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ahandfulofleaves.org/documents/Articles/The%20Four%20Noble%20Truths_Norman_PTS_2003.pdf" rel="nofollow">Four Noble Truths</a><br/>
* Tilman Vetter, <a class="external text" href="http://www.ahandfulofleaves.org/documents/The%20Ideas%20and%20Meditative%20Practices%20of%20Early%20Buddhism_Vetter.pdf" rel="nofollow">The Ideas and Meditative Practices of Early Buddhism, by Tilmann Vetter</a><br/>
* <cite class="citation book">Richard F. Gombrich (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=hQOAAgAAQBAJ" rel="nofollow">How Buddhism Began: The Conditioned Genesis of the Early Teachings</a>. Routledge. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-134-19639-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-134-19639-5">978-1-134-19639-5</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGautama+Buddha&amp;rft.au=Richard+F.+Gombrich&amp;rft.btitle=How+Buddhism+Began%3A+The+Conditioned+Genesis+of+the+Early+Teachings&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DhQOAAgAAQBAJ&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-134-19639-5&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>, chapter four<br/>
* <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFBronkhorst1993"><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bronkhorst" title="Johannes Bronkhorst">Bronkhorst, Johannes</a> (1993),</cite> The Two Traditions Of Meditation In Ancient India<i>, Motilal Banarsidass Publishers</i><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGautama+Buddha&amp;rft.aufirst=Johannes&amp;rft.aulast=Bronkhorst&amp;rft.btitle=The+Two+Traditions+Of+Meditation+In+Ancient+India&amp;rft.date=1993&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Motilal+Banarsidass+Publishers&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span>, chapter 7<br/>
* <cite class="citation" id="CITEREFAnderson1999">Anderson, Carol (1999),</cite> Pain and Its Ending: The Four Noble Truths in the Theravada Buddhist Canon<i>, Routledge</i><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGautama+Buddha&amp;rft.aufirst=Carol&amp;rft.aulast=Anderson&amp;rft.btitle=Pain+and+Its+Ending%3A+The+Four+Noble+Truths+in+the+Theravada+Buddhist+Canon&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Wayley1932-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Wayley1932_124-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Waley notes: <i>suukara-kanda</i>, "pig-bulb"; <i>suukara-paadika</i>, "pig's foot" and <i>sukaresh.ta</i> "sought-out by pigs". He cites Neumann's suggestion that if a plant called "sought-out by pigs" exists then <i>suukaramaddava</i> can mean "pig's delight".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The surviving portions of the scriptures of <a href="/wiki/Sarvastivada" title="Sarvastivada">Sarvastivada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mulasarvastivada" title="Mulasarvastivada">Mulasarvastivada</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahisasaka" title="Mahisasaka">Mahisasaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dharmaguptaka" title="Dharmaguptaka">Dharmaguptaka</a> and other schools,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988ix-138">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWarder1999_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWarder1999-139">[105]</a></sup> and the Chinese <a href="/wiki/%C4%80gama_(Buddhism)" title="Āgama (Buddhism)">Agamas</a> and other surviving portions of other early canons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Exemplary studies are the study on descriptions of "liberating insight" by Lambert Schmithausen,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmithausen1981-145">[110]</a></sup> the overview of early Buddhism by Tilmann Vetter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988-142">[107]</a></sup> the philological work on the four truths by K.R. Norman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1992_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1992-146">[111]</a></sup> the textual studies by Richard Gombrich,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGombrich1997-144">[109]</a></sup> and the research on early meditation methods by Johannes Bronkhorst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993-141">[106]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Well-known proponents of the first position are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.K._Warder" title="A.K. Warder">A.K. Warder</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-150">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to A.K. Warder, in his 1970 publication "Indian Buddhism"</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A proponent of the second position is Ronald Davidson.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ronald Davidson: "While most scholars agree that there was a rough body of sacred literature (disputed)(sic) that a relatively early community (disputed)(sic) maintained and transmitted, we have little confidence that much, if any, of surviving Buddhist scripture is actually the word of the historic Buddha."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson2003147_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson2003147-154">[115]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Well-known proponent of the third position are:<br/>
* J.W. de Jong: "It would be hypocritical to assert that nothing can be said about the doctrine of earliest Buddhism [...] the basic ideas of Buddhism found in the canonical writings could very well have been proclaimed by him [the Buddha], transmitted and developed by his disciples and, finally, codified in fixed formulas."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJong199325_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJong199325-156">[116]</a></sup><br/>
* Johannes Bronkhorst: "This position is to be preferred to (ii) for purely methodological reasons: only those who seek may find, even if no success is guaranteed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronkhorst1993vii-148">[112]</a></sup><br/>
* Donald Lopez: "The original teachings of the historical Buddha are extremely difficult, if not impossible, to recover or reconstruct."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopez19954_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopez19954-157">[117]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vetter: "However, if we look at the last, and in my opinion the most important, component of this list [the noble eightfold path], we are still dealing with what according to me is the real content of the middle way, dhyana-meditation, at least the stages two to four, which are said to be free of contemplation and reflection. Everything preceding the eighth part, i.e. right samadhi, apparently has the function of preparing for the right samadhi."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXX_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988XXX-161">[120]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Majjhima Nikaya 36</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-169">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Vetter: "I am especially thinking here of MN 26 (I p.163,32; 165,15;166,35) <i>kimkusalagavesi anuttaram santivarapadam pariyesamano</i> (searching for that which is beneficial, seeking the unsurpassable, best place of peace) and again MN 26 (passim), <i>anuttaramyagakkhemam nibbiinam pariyesati</i> (he seeks the unsurpassable safe place, the nirvana). <i>Anuppatta-sadattho</i> (one who has reached the right goal) is also a vague positive expression in the Arhatformula in MN 35 (I p, 235), see chapter 2, footnote 3, Furthermore, <i>satthi</i> (welfare) is important in e.g. SN 2.12 or 2.17 or Sn 269; and <i>sukha</i> and <i>rati</i> (happiness), in contrast to other places, as used in Sn 439 and 956. The oldest term was perhaps <i>amata</i> (immortal, immortality) [...] but one could say here that it is a negative term."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xv_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVetter1988xv-168">[125]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Understanding of these marks helps in the development of detachment:</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anicca" title="Anicca">Anicca</a> <i>(Sanskrit:</i> anitya<i>): That all things that come to be have an end;</i></span></li>
<li><span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/Dukkha" title="Dukkha">Dukkha</a></i> (Sanskrit: <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit Transliteration">duḥkha</span></i>): That nothing which comes to be is ultimately satisfying;</span></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Anatta" title="Anatta">Anattā</a></i> (Sanskrit: <i>anātman</i>): That nothing in the realm of experience can really be said to be "I" or "mine".</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-182"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-182">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"in Sanskrit philosophical literature, 'āstika' means 'one who believes in the authority of the Vedas', 'soul', 'Brahman'. ('nāstika' means the opposite of these).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-andrew_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-andrew-180">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gsghurye_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-gsghurye-181">[136]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
<dl>
<dt><i>Subnotes</i></dt>
</dl>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 15em; -webkit-column-width: 15em; column-width: 15em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some sources mention Kapilavastu as the birthplace of the Buddha. Gethin states: "The earliest Buddhist sources state that the future Buddha was born Siddhārtha Gautama (Pali Siddhattha Gotama), the son of a local chieftain — a <i>rājan</i> — in Kapilavastu (Pali Kapilavatthu) what is now the Indian–Nepalese border."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGethin199814_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGethin199814-56">[41]</a></sup> Gethin does not give references for this statement.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="background-color:#FFE8A6;"><a href="/wiki/Buddhist_cosmology" title="Buddhist cosmology">Cosmology</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
